<p><b>Kenya</b>, officially the <b>Republic of Kenya</b> (<a href="/wiki/Swahili_language" title="Swahili language">Swahili</a>: <i lang="sw">Jamhuri ya Kenya</i>), is a country in <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East Africa</a>. At 580,367 square kilometres (224,081 sq mi), Kenya is the world's 48th largest country by area. With a population of more than 47.6 million in the 2019 census,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Kenya is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_and_dependencies_by_population" title="List of countries and dependencies by population">29th most populous country</a> in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KNBS2019_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-KNBS2019-6">[6]</a></sup> Kenya's capital and largest city is <a href="/wiki/Nairobi" title="Nairobi">Nairobi</a>, while its oldest, currently second largest city, and first capital is the coastal city of <a href="/wiki/Mombasa" title="Mombasa">Mombasa</a>. <a href="/wiki/Kisumu" title="Kisumu">Kisumu City</a> is the third-largest city and also an inland port on <a href="/wiki/Lake_Victoria" title="Lake Victoria">Lake Victoria</a>. Other important urban centres include <a href="/wiki/Nakuru" title="Nakuru">Nakuru</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eldoret" title="Eldoret">Eldoret</a>. As of 2020, Kenya is the third-largest economy in sub-Saharan Africa after <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Kenya is bordered by <a href="/wiki/South_Sudan" title="South Sudan">South Sudan</a> to the northwest, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> to the north, <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a> to the east, <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a> to the west, <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a> to the south, and the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a> to the southeast. Its geography, climate and population vary widely, ranging from cold snow-capped mountaintops (Batian, Nelion and Point Lenana on <a href="/wiki/Mount_Kenya" title="Mount Kenya">Mount Kenya</a>) with vast surrounding forests, wildlife and fertile agricultural regions to temperate climates in western and rift valley counties and dry less fertile arid and semi-arid areas and absolute deserts (<a href="/wiki/Chalbi_Desert" title="Chalbi Desert">Chalbi Desert</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nyiri_Desert" title="Nyiri Desert">Nyiri Desert</a>).
</p><p>Kenya's earliest inhabitants were <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunter-gatherers</a>, like the present-day <a href="/wiki/Hadza_people" title="Hadza people">Hadza people</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> According to archaeological dating of associated artifacts and skeletal material, <a href="/wiki/South_Cushitic_languages" title="South Cushitic languages">Cushitic speakers</a> first settled in Kenya's lowlands between 3,200 and 1,300 BC, a phase known as the Lowland <a href="/wiki/Savanna_Pastoral_Neolithic" title="Savanna Pastoral Neolithic">Savanna Pastoral Neolithic</a>. <a href="/wiki/Nilo-Saharan_languages" title="Nilo-Saharan languages">Nilotic</a>-speaking <a href="/wiki/Pastoralism" title="Pastoralism">pastoralists</a> (ancestral to Kenya's Nilotic speakers) began migrating from present-day South Sudan into Kenya around 500 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EhretCHS_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-EhretCHS-16">[16]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Bantu people</a> settled at the coast and the interior between 250 BC and 500 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> European contact began in 1500 AD with the <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portuguese Empire</a>, and effective colonisation of Kenya began in the 19th century during the <a href="/wiki/European_exploration_of_Africa" title="European exploration of Africa">European exploration of the interior</a>. Modern-day Kenya emerged from <a href="/wiki/East_Africa_Protectorate" title="East Africa Protectorate">a protectorate</a> established by the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> in 1895 and the subsequent <a href="/wiki/Kenya_Colony" title="Kenya Colony">Kenya Colony</a>, which began in 1920. Numerous disputes between the UK and the colony led to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mau_Mau_uprising" title="Mau Mau uprising">Mau Mau revolution</a>, which began in 1952, and the declaration of independence in 1963. After independence, Kenya remained a member of the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth of Nations</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Kenya" title="Constitution of Kenya">current constitution</a> was adopted in 2010 and replaced the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Kenya_(1963)" title="Constitution of Kenya (1963)">1963 independence constitution</a>.
</p><p>Kenya is a <a href="/wiki/Presidential_system" title="Presidential system">presidential</a> <a href="/wiki/Representative_democracy" title="Representative democracy">representative democratic</a> republic, in which elected officials represent the people and the president is the head of state and government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Kenya is a member of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth of Nations</a>, <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a>, <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">International Monetary Fund</a>, <a href="/wiki/Common_Market_for_Eastern_and_Southern_Africa" title="Common Market for Eastern and Southern Africa">COMESA</a>, <a href="/wiki/International_Criminal_Court" title="International Criminal Court">International Criminal Court</a>, as well as other international organisations. With a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gross_National_Income" title="Gross National Income">GNI</a> of 1,840,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Kenya is a lower-middle-income economy. <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Kenya" title="Economy of Kenya">Kenya's economy</a> is the largest in eastern and central Africa,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto1_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-21">[21]</a></sup> with <a href="/wiki/Nairobi" title="Nairobi">Nairobi</a> serving as a major regional commercial hub.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto1_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-21">[21]</a></sup> Agriculture is the largest sector: tea and coffee are traditional cash crops, while fresh flowers are a fast-growing export. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Service_industry" title="Service industry">service industry</a> is also a major economic driver, particularly tourism. Kenya is a member of the <a href="/wiki/East_African_Community" title="East African Community">East African Community</a> trade bloc, though some international trade organisations categorise it as part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greater_Horn_of_Africa" title="Greater Horn of Africa">Greater Horn of Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Africa is Kenya's largest export market, followed by the European Union.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<p>The Republic of Kenya is named after <a href="/wiki/Mount_Kenya" title="Mount Kenya">Mount Kenya</a>. The earliest recorded version of the modern name was written by German explorer <a href="/wiki/Johann_Ludwig_Krapf" title="Johann Ludwig Krapf">Johann Ludwig Krapf</a> in the 19th century. While travelling with a Kamba caravan led by the legendary long-distance trader Chief Kivoi, Krapf spotted the mountain peak and asked what it was called. Kivoi told him "<i>Kĩ-Nyaa</i>" or "<i>Kĩĩma- Kĩĩnyaa</i>", probably because the pattern of black rock and white snow on its peaks reminded him of the feathers of the male ostrich.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sullivan_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-sullivan-24">[24]</a></sup> The Agikuyu, who inhabit the slopes of Mt. Kenya, call it Kĩrĩma Kĩrĩnyaga in <a href="/wiki/Kikuyu_language" title="Kikuyu language">Kikuyu</a>, while the Embu call it "Kirenyaa". All three names have the same meaning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ludwig Krapf recorded the name as both <i>Kenia</i> and <i>Kegnia</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krapf_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Krapf-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krapf_452_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-krapf_452-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-foottit_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-foottit-28">[28]</a></sup> Some have said that this was a precise notation of the African pronunciation <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/ɛ/: 'e' in 'dress'">ɛ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/j/: 'y' in 'yes'">j</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span>/</a></span></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> An 1882 map drawn by Joseph Thompsons, a Scottish geologist and naturalist, indicated Mt. Kenya as Mt. Kenia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sullivan_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-sullivan-24">[24]</a></sup> The mountain's name was accepted, <i><a href="/wiki/Pars_pro_toto" title="Pars pro toto">pars pro toto</a></i>, as the name of the country. It did not come into widespread official use during the early colonial period, when the country was referred to as the <a href="/wiki/East_Africa_Protectorate" title="East Africa Protectorate">East African Protectorate</a>. The official name was changed to the <a href="/wiki/Kenya_Colony" title="Kenya Colony">Colony of Kenya</a> in 1920.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Kenya" title="History of Kenya">History of Kenya</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For a chronological guide, see <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Kenya" title="Timeline of Kenya">Timeline of Kenya</a>.</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_prehistory">Human prehistory</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Turkana_Boy.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1437" data-file-width="504" decoding="async" height="399" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Turkana_Boy.jpg/140px-Turkana_Boy.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Turkana_Boy.jpg/210px-Turkana_Boy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Turkana_Boy.jpg/280px-Turkana_Boy.jpg 2x" width="140"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Turkana_Boy.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turkana_boy" title="Turkana boy">Turkana boy</a>, a 1.6-million-year-old <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hominid" title="Hominid">hominid</a> fossil belonging to <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a></i>.</div></div></div>
<p>Fossils found in Kenya have shown that primates inhabited the area for more than 20 million years. Recent findings near <a href="/wiki/Lake_Turkana" title="Lake Turkana">Lake Turkana</a> indicate that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hominid" title="Hominid">hominids</a> such as <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_habilis" title="Homo habilis">Homo habilis</a></i> (1.8 to 2.5 million years ago) and <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a></i> (1.9 million to 350,000 years ago) are possible direct ancestors of modern <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens</a></i>, and lived in Kenya in the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> epoch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Isaac_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Isaac-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>During excavations at Lake Turkana in 1984, paleoanthropologist <a href="/wiki/Richard_Leakey" title="Richard Leakey">Richard Leakey</a>, assisted by <a href="/wiki/Kamoya_Kimeu" title="Kamoya Kimeu">Kamoya Kimeu</a>, discovered the <a href="/wiki/Turkana_Boy" title="Turkana Boy">Turkana Boy</a>, a 1.6-million-year-old <i>Homo erectus</i> fossil. Previous research on early hominids is particularly identified with <a href="/wiki/Mary_Leakey" title="Mary Leakey">Mary Leakey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Louis_Leakey" title="Louis Leakey">Louis Leakey</a>, who were responsible for the preliminary archaeological research at <a href="/wiki/Olorgesailie" title="Olorgesailie">Olorgesailie</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hyrax_Hill" title="Hyrax Hill">Hyrax Hill</a>. Later work at the former site was undertaken by <a href="/wiki/Glynn_Isaac" title="Glynn Isaac">Glynn Isaac</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Isaac_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Isaac-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>East Africa, including Kenya, is one of the earliest regions where modern humans (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens</a></i>) are believed to have lived. Evidence was found in 2018, dating to about 320,000 years ago, at the Kenyan site of <a href="/wiki/Olorgesailie" title="Olorgesailie">Olorgesailie</a>, of the early emergence of <a href="/wiki/Behavioral_modernity" title="Behavioral modernity">modern behaviours</a>, including long-distance trade networks (involving goods such as obsidian), the use of pigments, and the possible making of projectile points. The authors of three 2018 studies on the site observed that the evidence of these behaviours is approximately contemporary to the earliest known <i>Homo sapiens</i> fossil remains (such as at <a href="/wiki/Jebel_Irhoud" title="Jebel Irhoud">Jebel Irhoud</a> in Morocco and <a href="/wiki/Florisbad_Skull" title="Florisbad Skull">Florisbad</a> in South Africa), and they suggest that complex and modern behaviours had already begun in Africa around the time of the emergence of <i>Homo sapiens</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NPR-593591796_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-NPR-593591796-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Atlantic-555674_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Atlantic-555674-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brooks_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brooks-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Neolithic">Neolithic</span></h3>
<p>The first inhabitants of present-day Kenya were <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunter-gatherer</a> groups, akin to the modern <a href="/wiki/Khoisan" title="Khoisan">Khoisan</a> speakers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EhretC18_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-EhretC18-34">[34]</a></sup> These people were later largely replaced by agropastoralist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cushitic" title="Cushitic">Cushitic</a> (ancestral to Kenya's Cushitic speakers) from the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EhretHRSC_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-EhretHRSC-35">[35]</a></sup> During the early <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a>, the regional climate shifted from dry to wetter conditions, providing an opportunity for the development of cultural traditions such as agriculture and <a href="/wiki/Herding" title="Herding">herding</a>, in a more favourable environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EhretC18_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-EhretC18-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>Around 500 BC, <a href="/wiki/Nilo-Saharan_languages" title="Nilo-Saharan languages">Nilotic</a>-speaking <a href="/wiki/Pastoralism" title="Pastoralism">pastoralists</a> (ancestral to Kenya's Nilotic speakers) started migrating from present-day southern Sudan into Kenya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EhretCHS_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-EhretCHS-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AmbroseALR_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-AmbroseALR-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AmbroseSG_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-AmbroseSG-37">[37]</a></sup> Nilotic groups in Kenya include the <a href="/wiki/Kalenjin_people" title="Kalenjin people">Kalenjin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samburu_people" title="Samburu people">Samburu</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Luo_(family_of_ethnic_groups)" title="Luo (family of ethnic groups)">Luo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkana_people" title="Turkana people">Turkana</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Maasai_people" title="Maasai people">Maasai</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ILO_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-ILO-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the first millennium AD, <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Bantu</a>-speaking farmers had moved into the region, initially along the coast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EhretACA_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-EhretACA-39">[39]</a></sup> The Bantus originated in West Africa along the <a href="/wiki/Benue_River" title="Benue River">Benue River</a> in what is now eastern <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a> and western <a href="/wiki/Cameroon" title="Cameroon">Cameroon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WayneSmith_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-WayneSmith-40">[40]</a></sup> The Bantu migration brought new developments in agriculture and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_ferrous_metallurgy" title="History of ferrous metallurgy">ironworking</a> to the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WayneSmith_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-WayneSmith-40">[40]</a></sup> Bantu groups in Kenya include the <a href="/wiki/Kikuyu_people" title="Kikuyu people">Kikuyu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Luhya_people" title="Luhya people">Luhya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kamba_people" title="Kamba people">Kamba</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gusii_people" title="Gusii people">Kisii</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meru_people" title="Meru people">Meru</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kuria_people" title="Kuria people">Kuria</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aembu" title="Aembu">Aembu</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ambeere" title="Ambeere">Ambeere</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wadawida" title="Wadawida">Wadawida</a>-Watuweta, Wapokomo, and <a href="/wiki/Mijikenda_peoples" title="Mijikenda peoples">Mijikenda</a>, among others.
</p><p>Notable prehistoric sites in the interior of Kenya include the (possibly archaeoastronomical) site <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Namoratunga" title="Namoratunga">Namoratunga</a> on the west side of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Turkana" title="Lake Turkana">Lake Turkana</a> and the walled settlement of <a href="/wiki/Thimlich_Ohinga" title="Thimlich Ohinga">Thimlich Ohinga</a> in <a href="/wiki/Migori_County" title="Migori County">Migori County</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Swahili_trade_period">Swahili trade period</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Swahili_culture" title="Swahili culture">Swahili culture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Zanzibar" title="Sultanate of Zanzibar">Sultanate of Zanzibar</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lamu_door.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2012" data-file-width="1444" decoding="async" height="307" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Lamu_door.jpg/220px-Lamu_door.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Lamu_door.jpg/330px-Lamu_door.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Lamu_door.jpg/440px-Lamu_door.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lamu_door.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A traditional <a href="/wiki/Swahili_culture" title="Swahili culture">Swahili</a> carved wooden door in <a href="/wiki/Lamu" title="Lamu">Lamu</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The Kenyan coast had served host to communities of <a href="/wiki/Ironwork" title="Ironwork">ironworkers</a> and Bantu subsistence farmers, hunters, and fishers who supported the economy with agriculture, fishing, metal production, and trade with foreign countries. These communities formed the earliest city-states in the region, which were collectively known as <a href="/wiki/Azania" title="Azania">Azania</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pbs.org_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-pbs.org-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the 1st century CE, many of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/City-states" title="City-states">city-states</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Mombasa" title="Mombasa">Mombasa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malindi" title="Malindi">Malindi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a> began to establish trading relations with <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabs</a>. This led to increased economic growth of the Swahili states, the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> influences on the Swahili <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bantu_language" title="Bantu language">Bantu language</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cultural_diffusion" title="Cultural diffusion">cultural diffusion</a>, as well as the Swahili city-states becoming members of a larger trade network.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nanjira_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nanjira-43">[43]</a></sup> Many historians had long believed that the city-states were established by Arab or Persian traders, but archaeological evidence has led scholars to recognise the city-states as an indigenous development which, though subjected to foreign influence due to trade, retained a Bantu cultural core.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Kilwa_Sultanate" title="Kilwa Sultanate">Kilwa Sultanate</a> was a medieval <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sultanate" title="Sultanate">sultanate</a> centred at <a href="/wiki/Kilwa_Kisiwani" title="Kilwa Kisiwani">Kilwa</a>, in modern-day Tanzania. At its height, its authority stretched over the entire length of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swahili_Coast" title="Swahili Coast">Swahili Coast</a>, including Kenya. It was said to be founded in the 10th century by <a href="/wiki/Ali_ibn_al-Hassan_Shirazi" title="Ali ibn al-Hassan Shirazi">Ali ibn al-Hassan Shirazi</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">Persian</a> Sultan from <a href="/wiki/Shiraz" title="Shiraz">Shiraz</a> in southern Iran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> However, scholars have suggested that claims of Arab or Persian origin of city-states were attempts by the <a href="/wiki/Swahili_people" title="Swahili people">Swahili</a> to legitimise themselves both locally and internationally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Since the 10th century, rulers of Kilwa would go on to build elaborate coral mosques and introduce copper coinage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>Swahili, a Bantu language with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>, and other Middle-Eastern and South Asian <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">loanwords</a>, later developed as a <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i> for trade between the different peoples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pbs.org_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-pbs.org-41">[41]</a></sup> Since the turn into the 20th century, Swahili has adopted numerous loanwords and calques from English, many of them originating during English colonial rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Portuguese_colonization">Early Portuguese colonization</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Braun_Mombasa_UBHD.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="629" data-file-width="706" decoding="async" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Braun_Mombasa_UBHD.jpg/220px-Braun_Mombasa_UBHD.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Braun_Mombasa_UBHD.jpg/330px-Braun_Mombasa_UBHD.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Braun_Mombasa_UBHD.jpg/440px-Braun_Mombasa_UBHD.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Braun_Mombasa_UBHD.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portuguese presence</a> in Kenya lasted from 1498 until 1730. <a href="/wiki/Mombasa" title="Mombasa">Mombasa</a> was under Portuguese rule from 1593 to 1698 and again from 1728 to 1729.</div></div></div>
<p>The Swahili built Mombasa into a major port city and established trade links with other nearby city-states, as well as commercial centres in Persia, Arabia, and even India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> By the 15th-century, Portuguese voyager <a href="/wiki/Duarte_Barbosa" title="Duarte Barbosa">Duarte Barbosa</a> claimed that "Mombasa is a place of great traffic and has a good harbour in which there are always moored small craft of many kinds and also great ships, both of which are bound from Sofala and others which come from Cambay and Melinde and others which sail to the island of Zanzibar."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 17th century, the Swahili coast was conquered and came under the direct rule of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Omani_Arabs" title="Omani Arabs">Omani Arabs</a>, who expanded the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean_slave_trade" title="Indian Ocean slave trade">slave trade</a> to meet the demands of plantations in <a href="/wiki/Oman" title="Oman">Oman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Initially, these traders came mainly from Oman, but later many came from Zanzibar (such as <a href="/wiki/Tippu_Tip" title="Tippu Tip">Tippu Tip</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> In addition, the Portuguese started buying slaves from the Omani and Zanzibari traders in response to the interruption of the transatlantic slave trade by British abolitionists.
</p><p>Throughout the centuries, the Kenyan coast has played host to many merchants and explorers. Among the cities that line the Kenyan coast is Malindi. It has remained an important Swahili settlement since the 14th century and once rivalled Mombasa for dominance in the African Great Lakes region. Malindi has traditionally been a friendly port city for foreign powers. In 1414, the Chinese trader and explorer <a href="/wiki/Zheng_He" title="Zheng He">Zheng He</a>, representing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_Dynasty" title="Ming Dynasty">Ming Dynasty</a>, visited the East African coast <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treasure_voyages" title="Treasure voyages">on one of his last 'treasure voyages'</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Malindi authorities also welcomed the Portuguese explorer <a href="/wiki/Vasco_da_Gama" title="Vasco da Gama">Vasco da Gama</a> in 1498.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="18th_and_19th_centuries">18th and 19th centuries</span></h3>
<p>During the 18th and 19th century C.E, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Masai_people" title="Masai people">Masai people</a> moved into what is now modern-day central Kenya, from a region north of Lake Rudolf (now <a href="/wiki/Lake_Turkana" title="Lake Turkana">Lake Turkana</a>). Although there were not many, they managed to conquer a great amount of Bantu-speaking peoples, who did not put up much resistance. The <a href="/wiki/Nandi_people" title="Nandi people">Nandi</a> peoples managed to oppose the Masai, while the Taveta peoples fled to the forests on the eastern edge of <a href="/wiki/Mount_Kilimanjaro" title="Mount Kilimanjaro">Mount Kilimanjaro</a>, along with the Kikuyu peoples, although they later were forced to leave the land due to the threat of <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a>. An outbreak of either <a href="/wiki/Rinderpest" title="Rinderpest">rinderpest</a> or <a href="/wiki/Pleuropneumonia" title="Pleuropneumonia">pleuropneumonia</a> greatly affected the Masai's cattle, while an epidemic of smallpox affected the Masai themselves. After the death of the Masai <a href="/wiki/Mbatian" title="Mbatian">Mbatian</a>, the chief <i>laibon</i> (medicine man), the Masai split into warring factions. There was much strife between the Nilotic (Masai) and Bantu peoples; however, cooperation between such groups as the <a href="/wiki/Luo_people" title="Luo people">Luo people</a>, <a href="/wiki/Luhya_people" title="Luhya people">Luhya people</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gusii_people" title="Gusii people">Gusii people</a> is evinced by shared vocabulary for modern implements and similar economic regimes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Although Arab traders remained in the area, trade routes were disrupted by the hostile Masai, though there was trade in ivory between these factions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> The first foreigners to successfully get past the Masai were <a href="/wiki/Johann_Ludwig_Krapf" title="Johann Ludwig Krapf">Johann Ludwig Krapf</a> and <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Rebmann" title="Johannes Rebmann">Johannes Rebmann</a>, two German missionaries who established a mission in <a href="/wiki/Rabai" title="Rabai">Rabai</a>, not too far from <a href="/wiki/Mombasa" title="Mombasa">Mombasa</a>. The pair were the first Europeans to sight <a href="/wiki/Mount_Kenya" title="Mount Kenya">Mount Kenya</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="British_Kenya_.281888.E2.80.931962.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="British_Kenya_(1888–1962)">British Kenya (1888–1962)</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kenya_Colony" title="Kenya Colony">Kenya Colony</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Africa_1909_16a.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="679" data-file-width="610" decoding="async" height="245" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Africa_1909_16a.png/220px-Africa_1909_16a.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Africa_1909_16a.png/330px-Africa_1909_16a.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Africa_1909_16a.png/440px-Africa_1909_16a.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Africa_1909_16a.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_East_Africa" title="British East Africa">British East Africa</a> in 1909</div></div></div>
<p>The colonial history of Kenya dates from the establishment of a <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German</a> protectorate over the Sultan of <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a>'s coastal possessions in 1885, followed by the arrival of the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_British_East_Africa_Company" title="Imperial British East Africa Company">Imperial British East Africa Company</a> in 1888. Imperial rivalry was prevented when Germany handed its coastal holdings to Britain in 1890. This was followed by the building of the <a href="/wiki/Uganda_Railway" title="Uganda Railway">Uganda Railway</a> passing through the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclopædia_Britannica_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopædia_Britannica-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>The building of the railway was resisted by some ethnic groups—notably the <a href="/wiki/Nandi_people" title="Nandi people">Nandi</a>, led by <i><a href="/wiki/Orkoiyot" title="Orkoiyot">Orkoiyot</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Koitalel_Arap_Samoei" title="Koitalel Arap Samoei">Koitalel Arap Samoei</a> from 1890 to 1900—but the British eventually built it. The <i>Nandi</i> were the first ethnic group to be put in a native reserve to stop them from disrupting the building of the railway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclopædia_Britannica_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopædia_Britannica-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the railway construction era, there was a significant influx of Indian workers, who provided the bulk of the skilled manpower required for construction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R._Mugo_Gatheru_2005_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-R._Mugo_Gatheru_2005-60">[60]</a></sup> They and most of their descendants later remained in Kenya and formed the core of several distinct Indian communities, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ismailism" title="Ismailism">Ismaili Muslim</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sikh" title="Sikh">Sikh</a> communities. While building the railway through <a href="/wiki/Tsavo" title="Tsavo">Tsavo</a>, a number of the Indian railway workers and local African labourers were attacked by two lions known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tsavo_maneaters" title="Tsavo maneaters">Tsavo maneaters</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>At the outbreak of <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> in August 1914, the governors of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_East_Africa" title="British East Africa">British East Africa</a> (as the protectorate was generally known) and <a href="/wiki/German_East_Africa" title="German East Africa">German East Africa</a> initially agreed on a truce in an attempt to keep the young colonies out of direct hostilities. But Lieutenant Colonel <a href="/wiki/Paul_von_Lettow-Vorbeck" title="Paul von Lettow-Vorbeck">Paul von Lettow-Vorbeck</a>, the German military commander, determined to tie down as many British resources as possible. Completely cut off from Germany, Lettow-Vorbeck conducted <a href="/wiki/East_African_campaign_(World_War_I)" title="East African campaign (World War I)">an effective guerrilla warfare campaign</a>, living off the land, capturing British supplies, and remaining undefeated. He eventually surrendered in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Rhodesia" title="Northern Rhodesia">Northern Rhodesia</a> (today Zambia) 14 days after the Armistice was signed in 1918.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R._Mugo_Gatheru_2005_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-R._Mugo_Gatheru_2005-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kurve_bei_Mombasa.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="339" data-file-width="821" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Kurve_bei_Mombasa.jpg/300px-Kurve_bei_Mombasa.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Kurve_bei_Mombasa.jpg/450px-Kurve_bei_Mombasa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Kurve_bei_Mombasa.jpg/600px-Kurve_bei_Mombasa.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kurve_bei_Mombasa.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Uganda_Railway" title="Uganda Railway">Kenya–Uganda Railway</a> near <a href="/wiki/Mombasa" title="Mombasa">Mombasa</a>, about 1899.</div></div></div>
<p>To chase von Lettow, the British deployed the <a href="/wiki/British_Indian_Army" title="British Indian Army">British Indian Army</a> troops from India but needed large numbers of porters to overcome the formidable logistics of transporting supplies far into the interior on foot. The <a href="/wiki/Carrier_Corps" title="Carrier Corps">Carrier Corps</a> was formed and ultimately mobilised over 400,000 Africans, contributing to their long-term politicisation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R._Mugo_Gatheru_2005_60-2"><a href="#cite_note-R._Mugo_Gatheru_2005-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1920, the East Africa Protectorate was turned into a colony and renamed Kenya after its highest mountain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclopædia_Britannica_59-2"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopædia_Britannica-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the early part of the 20th century, the interior central highlands were settled by British and other European farmers, who became wealthy farming coffee and tea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> One depiction of this period of change from a colonist's perspective is found in the memoir <i><a href="/wiki/Out_of_Africa" title="Out of Africa">Out of Africa</a></i> by Danish author Baroness Karen von Blixen-Finecke, published in 1937. By the 1930s, approximately 30,000 white settlers lived in the area and gained a political voice because of their contribution to the market economy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R._Mugo_Gatheru_2005_60-3"><a href="#cite_note-R._Mugo_Gatheru_2005-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>The central highlands were already home to over a million members of the Kikuyu people, most of whom had no land claims in European terms and lived as itinerant farmers. To protect their interests, the settlers banned the growing of coffee and introduced a hut tax, and the landless were granted less and less land in exchange for their labour. A massive exodus to the cities ensued as their ability to make a living from the land dwindled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R._Mugo_Gatheru_2005_60-4"><a href="#cite_note-R._Mugo_Gatheru_2005-60">[60]</a></sup> By the 1950s, there were 80,000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Whites_in_Kenya" title="Whites in Kenya">white settlers</a> living in Kenya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>Throughout <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kenya_in_World_War_II" title="Kenya in World War II">Kenya was</a> an important source of manpower and agriculture for the United Kingdom. Kenya itself was the site of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East_African_Campaign_(World_War_II)" title="East African Campaign (World War II)">fighting</a> between Allied forces and Italian troops in 1940–41, when Italian forces invaded. <a href="/wiki/Wajir" title="Wajir">Wajir</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malindi" title="Malindi">Malindi</a> were bombed as well.
</p><p>In 1952, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_II" title="Queen Elizabeth II">Princess Elizabeth</a> and her husband <a href="/wiki/Prince_Philip,_Duke_of_Edinburgh" title="Prince Philip, Duke of Edinburgh">Prince Philip</a> were on holiday at the <a href="/wiki/Treetops_Hotel" title="Treetops Hotel">Treetops Hotel</a> in Kenya when her father, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_George_VI" title="King George VI">King George VI</a>, died in his sleep. Elizabeth cut short her trip and returned home immediately to assume the throne. She was crowned Queen Elizabeth II at Westminster Abbey in 1953 and as British hunter and conservationist <a href="/wiki/Jim_Corbett" title="Jim Corbett">Jim Corbett</a> (who accompanied the royal couple) put it, she went up a tree in Africa a princess and came down a queen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mau_Mau_Uprising">Mau Mau Uprising</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mau_Mau_Uprising" title="Mau Mau Uprising">Mau Mau Uprising</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Statue_of_Dedan_Kimathi_Nairobi,_Kenya.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2176" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Statue_of_Dedan_Kimathi_Nairobi%2C_Kenya.jpg/220px-Statue_of_Dedan_Kimathi_Nairobi%2C_Kenya.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Statue_of_Dedan_Kimathi_Nairobi%2C_Kenya.jpg/330px-Statue_of_Dedan_Kimathi_Nairobi%2C_Kenya.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Statue_of_Dedan_Kimathi_Nairobi%2C_Kenya.jpg/440px-Statue_of_Dedan_Kimathi_Nairobi%2C_Kenya.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Statue_of_Dedan_Kimathi_Nairobi,_Kenya.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A statue of <a href="/wiki/Dedan_Kimathi" title="Dedan Kimathi">Dedan Kimathi</a>, a Kenyan rebel leader with the <a href="/wiki/Kenya_Land_and_Freedom_Army" title="Kenya Land and Freedom Army">Mau Mau</a> who fought against the British colonial system in the 1950s.</div></div></div>
<p>From October 1952 to December 1959, Kenya was in a state of emergency arising from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mau_Mau_Uprising" title="Mau Mau Uprising">Mau Mau rebellion</a> against British rule. The Mau Mau, also known as the Kenya Land and Freedom Army, were primarily Kikuyu people. During the colonial administration's crackdown, over 11,000 rebel fighters had been killed, along with 100 British troops and 2000 Kenyan loyalist soldiers. The governor requested and obtained British and African troops, including the <a href="/wiki/King%27s_African_Rifles" title="King's African Rifles">King's African Rifles</a>. The British began counter-insurgency operations. In May 1953, General Sir <a href="/wiki/George_Erskine" title="George Erskine">George Erskine</a> took charge as commander-in-chief of the colony's armed forces, with the personal backing of <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wunyabari_O._Maloba_1993_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wunyabari_O._Maloba_1993-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>The capture of <a href="/wiki/Waruhiu_Itote" title="Waruhiu Itote">Waruhiu Itote</a> (nom de guerre <i>"General China"</i>) on 15 January 1954 and the subsequent interrogation led to a better understanding of the Mau Mau command structure for the British. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mau_Mau_Uprising#British_gain_the_initiative" title="Mau Mau Uprising">Operation Anvil</a> opened on 24 April 1954, after weeks of planning by the army with the approval of the War Council. The operation effectively placed Nairobi under military siege. Nairobi's occupants were screened and suspected Mau Mau supporters moved to detention camps. More than 80,000 Kikuyu were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_British_Detention_Camps_during_the_Mau_Mau_Uprising" title="List of British Detention Camps during the Mau Mau Uprising">held in detention camps</a> without trial, often subject to brutal treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> The Home Guard formed the core of the government's strategy as it was composed of loyalist Africans, not foreign forces such as the <a href="/wiki/British_Army" title="British Army">British Army</a> and King's African Rifles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>The capture of <a href="/wiki/Dedan_Kimathi" title="Dedan Kimathi">Dedan Kimathi</a> on 21 October 1956 in <a href="/wiki/Nyeri" title="Nyeri">Nyeri</a> signified the ultimate defeat of the Mau Mau and essentially ended the military offensive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wunyabari_O._Maloba_1993_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wunyabari_O._Maloba_1993-65">[65]</a></sup> During this period, substantial governmental changes to land tenure occurred. The most important of these was the <a href="/wiki/Swynnerton_Plan" title="Swynnerton Plan">Swynnerton Plan</a>, which was used to both reward loyalists and punish Mau Mau. This left roughly 1/3rd of Kikuyu bereft of any tenancy land arrangement and thus propertyless at the time of independence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Somalis_of_Kenya_referendum.2C_1962"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Somalis_of_Kenya_referendum,_1962">Somalis of Kenya referendum, 1962</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Somalis_in_Kenya" title="Somalis in Kenya">Somalis in Kenya</a></div>
<p>Before Kenya got its independence, <a href="/wiki/Somalis_in_Kenya" title="Somalis in Kenya">Somali ethnic people in present-day Kenya</a> in the areas of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_Frontier_Districts" title="Northern Frontier Districts">Northern Frontier Districts</a> petitioned Her Majesty's Government not to be included in Kenya. The colonial government decided to hold Kenya's first referendum in 1962 to check the willingness of <a href="/wiki/Somalis_in_Kenya" title="Somalis in Kenya">Somalis in Kenya</a> to join <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>The result of the referendum showed that 86% of Somalis in Kenya wanted to join <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a>, but the British colonial administration rejected the result and the Somalis remained in Kenya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Independence">Independence</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jomo_Kenyatta.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="366" data-file-width="255" decoding="async" height="244" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Jomo_Kenyatta.jpg/170px-Jomo_Kenyatta.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c2/Jomo_Kenyatta.jpg 1.5x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jomo_Kenyatta.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The first president and founding father of Kenya, <a href="/wiki/Jomo_Kenyatta" title="Jomo Kenyatta">Jomo Kenyatta</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The first direct elections for native Kenyans to the Legislative Council took place in 1957.
</p><p>Despite British hopes of handing power to "moderate" local rivals, it was the <a href="/wiki/Kenya_African_National_Union" title="Kenya African National Union">Kenya African National Union</a> (KANU) of <a href="/wiki/Jomo_Kenyatta" title="Jomo Kenyatta">Jomo Kenyatta</a> that formed a government. The Colony of Kenya and the Protectorate of Kenya each came to an end on 12 December 1963, with independence conferred on all of Kenya. The U.K. ceded sovereignty over the Colony of Kenya. The Sultan of Zanzibar agreed that simultaneous with independence for the colony, he would cease to have sovereignty over the Protectorate of Kenya so that all of Kenya would become one sovereign state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kenneth_Roberts-Wray_1966._P._762_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kenneth_Roberts-Wray_1966._P._762-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> In this way, Kenya became an independent country under the Kenya Independence Act 1963 of the United Kingdom. On 12 December 1964, Kenya became a republic under the name "Republic of Kenya".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kenneth_Roberts-Wray_1966._P._762_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kenneth_Roberts-Wray_1966._P._762-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>Concurrently, the Kenyan army fought the <a href="/wiki/Shifta_War" title="Shifta War">Shifta War</a> against ethnic Somali rebels inhabiting the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_Frontier_District" title="Northern Frontier District">Northern Frontier District</a> who wanted to join their kin in the <a href="/wiki/Somali_Republic" title="Somali Republic">Somali Republic</a> to the north.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> A ceasefire was eventually reached with the signing of the Arusha Memorandum in October 1967, but relative insecurity prevailed through 1969.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hogg1986_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hogg1986-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Howell1968_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Howell1968-76">[76]</a></sup> To discourage further invasions, Kenya signed a defence pact with <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> in 1969, which is still in effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_presidency">First presidency</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Jomo_Kenyatta" title="Presidency of Jomo Kenyatta">Presidency of Jomo Kenyatta</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jomo_Kenyatta" title="Jomo Kenyatta">Jomo Kenyatta</a></div>
<p>On 12 December 1964, the Republic of Kenya was proclaimed, and <a href="/wiki/Jomo_Kenyatta" title="Jomo Kenyatta">Jomo Kenyatta</a> became Kenya's first president.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Under Kenyatta, corruption became widespread throughout the government, civil service, and business community. Kenyatta and his family were tied up with this corruption as they enriched themselves through the mass purchase of property after 1963. Their acquisitions in the Central, Rift Valley, and Coast Provinces aroused great anger among landless Kenyans. His family used his presidential position to circumvent legal or administrative obstacles to acquiring property. The Kenyatta family also heavily invested in the coastal hotel business, with Kenyatta personally owning the Leonard Beach Hotel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>Kenyatta's mixed legacy was highlighted at the 10-year anniversary of Kenya's independence. A December 1973 article in <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> praised Kenyatta's leadership and Kenya for emerging as a model of pragmatism and conservatism. Kenya's GDP had increased at an annual rate of 6.6%, higher than the population growth rate of more than 3%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> But <a href="/wiki/Amnesty_International" title="Amnesty International">Amnesty International</a> responded to the article by stating the cost of the stability in terms of human rights abuses. The opposition party started by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oginga_Odinga" title="Oginga Odinga">Oginga Odinga</a>—<a href="/wiki/Kenya_People%27s_Union" title="Kenya People's Union">Kenya People's Union</a> (KPU)—was banned in 1969 after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kisumu_Massacre" title="Kisumu Massacre">Kisumu Massacre</a> and KPU leaders were still in detention without trial in gross violation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.N._Declaration_of_Human_Rights" title="U.N. Declaration of Human Rights">U.N. Declaration of Human Rights</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sproul_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-sproul-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> The Kenya Students Union, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jehovah_Witnesses" title="Jehovah Witnesses">Jehovah Witnesses</a> and all opposition parties were outlawed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sproul_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-sproul-81">[81]</a></sup> Kenyatta ruled until his death on 22 August 1978.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Moi_era">Moi era</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moi_and_Bush.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="221" data-file-width="298" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Moi_and_Bush.jpg/220px-Moi_and_Bush.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/24/Moi_and_Bush.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moi_and_Bush.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Daniel_arap_Moi" title="Daniel arap Moi">Daniel arap Moi</a>, Kenya's second President, and George W. Bush, 2001</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Daniel_arap_Moi" title="Daniel arap Moi">Daniel arap Moi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Daniel_Moi" title="Presidency of Daniel Moi">Presidency of Daniel Moi</a>, <a href="/wiki/1978_Kenyan_presidential_election" title="1978 Kenyan presidential election">1978 Kenyan presidential election</a>, <a href="/wiki/1988_Kenyan_general_election" title="1988 Kenyan general election">1988 Kenyan general election</a>, and <a href="/wiki/1992_Kenyan_general_election" title="1992 Kenyan general election">1992 Kenyan general election</a></div>
<p>After Kenyatta died, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_arap_Moi" title="Daniel arap Moi">Daniel arap Moi</a> became president. He retained the presidency, running unopposed in elections held in 1979, 1983 (<a href="/wiki/Snap_election" title="Snap election">snap elections</a>), and 1988, all of which were held under the single-party constitution. The 1983 elections were held a year early, and were a direct result of <a href="/wiki/1982_Kenyan_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat_attempt" title="1982 Kenyan coup d'état attempt">a failed military coup</a> on 2 August 1982.
</p><p>The 1982 coup was masterminded by a low-ranking Air Force serviceman, Senior Private <a href="/wiki/Hezekiah_Ochuka" title="Hezekiah Ochuka">Hezekiah Ochuka</a>, and was staged mainly by enlisted men of the Air Force. It was quickly suppressed by forces commanded by Chief of General Staff <a href="/wiki/Mahamoud_Mohamed" title="Mahamoud Mohamed">Mahamoud Mohamed</a>, a veteran Somali military official.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> They included the General Service Unit (GSU)—a paramilitary wing of the police—and later the regular police.
</p><p>On the heels of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Garissa_Massacre" title="Garissa Massacre">Garissa Massacre</a> of 1980, Kenyan troops committed the <a href="/wiki/Wagalla_massacre" title="Wagalla massacre">Wagalla massacre</a> in 1984 against thousands of civilians in <a href="/wiki/Wajir_County" title="Wajir County">Wajir County</a>. An official probe into the atrocities was later ordered in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (December 2019)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The election held in 1988 saw the advent of the <i>mlolongo</i> (queuing) system, where voters were supposed to line up behind their favoured candidates instead of casting a secret ballot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> This was seen as the climax of a very undemocratic regime and led to widespread agitation for constitutional reform. Several contentious clauses, including the one that allowed for only one political party, were changed in the following years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transition_to_multiparty_democracy">Transition to multiparty democracy</span></h3>
<p>In 1991, Kenya transitioned to a multiparty political system after 26 years of single-party rule. On 28 October 1992, Moi dissolved parliament, five months before the end of his term. As a result, preparations began for all elective seats in parliament as well as the president. The election was scheduled to take place on 7 December 1992, but delays led to its postponement to 29 December. Apart from KANU, the ruling party, other parties represented in the elections included <a href="/wiki/Forum_for_the_Restoration_of_Democracy" title="Forum for the Restoration of Democracy">FORD</a> Kenya and FORD Asili. This election was marked by large-scale intimidation of opponents and harassment of election officials. It resulted in an economic crisis propagated by ethnic violence as the president was accused of rigging electoral results to retain power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keith_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keith-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldsworthy_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goldsworthy-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ParliamentKe_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-ParliamentKe-90">[90]</a></sup> This election was a turning point for Kenya as it signified the beginning of the end of Moi's leadership and the rule of KANU. Moi retained the presidency and <a href="/wiki/George_Saitoti" title="George Saitoti">George Saitoti</a> became vice president. Although it held on to power, KANU won 100 seats and lost 88 seats to the six opposition parties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keith_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-Keith-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ParliamentKe_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-ParliamentKe-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable">

<p>The 1992 elections marked the beginning of multiparty politics after more than 25 years of KANU rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keith_88-2"><a href="#cite_note-Keith-88">[88]</a></sup> Following skirmishes in the aftermath of the elections, 5,000 people were killed and another 75,000 displaced from their homes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> In the next five years, many political alliances were formed in preparation for the next elections. In 1994, <a href="/wiki/Jaramogi_Oginga_Odinga" title="Jaramogi Oginga Odinga">Jaramogi Oginga Odinga</a> died and several coalitions joined his FORD Kenya party to form a new party, United National Democratic Alliance. This party was plagued with disagreements. In 1995, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Leakey" title="Richard Leakey">Richard Leakey</a> formed the Safina party, but it was denied registration until November 1997.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCKenyaHist_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBCKenyaHist-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1996, KANU revised the constitution to allow Moi to remain president for another term. Subsequently, Moi stood for reelection and won a 5th term in 1997.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> His win was strongly criticised by his major opponents, <a href="/wiki/Mwai_Kibaki" title="Mwai Kibaki">Kibaki</a> and <a href="/wiki/Raila_Odinga" title="Raila Odinga">Odinga</a>, as fraudulent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCKenyaHist_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-BBCKenyaHist-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-94">[94]</a></sup> Following this win, Moi was constitutionally barred from another presidential term. Beginning in 1998, he attempted to influence the country's succession politics to have <a href="/wiki/Uhuru_Kenyatta" title="Uhuru Kenyatta">Uhuru Kenyatta</a> elected in the 2002 elections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="President_Kibaki_and_the_road_to_a_new_constitution">President Kibaki and the road to a new constitution</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Mwai_Kibaki" title="Mwai Kibaki">Mwai Kibaki</a>, <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Mwai_Kibaki" title="Presidency of Mwai Kibaki">Presidency of Mwai Kibaki</a>, <a href="/wiki/2002_Kenyan_general_election" title="2002 Kenyan general election">2002 Kenyan general election</a>, and <a href="/wiki/2007_Kenyan_general_election" title="2007 Kenyan general election">2007 Kenyan general election</a></div>
<p>Moi's plan to be replaced by Uhuru Kenyatta failed, and <a href="/wiki/Mwai_Kibaki" title="Mwai Kibaki">Mwai Kibaki</a>, running for the opposition coalition "National Rainbow Coalition" (<a href="/wiki/National_Rainbow_Coalition" title="National Rainbow Coalition">NARC</a>), was elected president. David Anderson (2003) reports the elections were judged free and fair by local and international observers, and seemed to mark a turning point in Kenya's democratic evolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2005, Kenyans rejected a plan to replace the 1963 independence constitution with a new one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> As a result, the elections of 2007 took place following the procedure set by the old constitution. Kibaki was reelected in highly contested elections marred by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2007%E2%80%9308_Kenyan_crisis" title="2007–08 Kenyan crisis">political and ethnic violence</a>. The main opposition leader, Raila Odinga, claimed the election was rigged and that he was the rightfully elected president. In the ensuing violence, 1,500 people were killed and another 600,000 internally displaced, making it the worst post-election violence in Kenya. To stop the death and displacement of people, Kibaki and Odinga agreed to work together, with the latter taking the position of a prime minister.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> This made Odinga <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Kenya" title="Prime Minister of Kenya">the second prime minister of Kenya</a>.
</p><p>In July 2010, Kenya partnered with other East African countries to form the new East African Common Market within the <a href="/wiki/East_African_Community" title="East African Community">East African Community</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> In 2011, <a href="/wiki/Operation_Linda_Nchi" title="Operation Linda Nchi">Kenya began sending troops</a> to Somalia to fight the terror group <a href="/wiki/Al-Shabaab_(militant_group)" title="Al-Shabaab (militant group)">Al-Shabaab</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> In mid-2011, two consecutive missed rainy seasons precipitated the worst <a href="/wiki/2011_East_Africa_drought" title="2011 East Africa drought">drought in East Africa</a> in 60 years. The northwestern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turkana_District" title="Turkana District">Turkana region</a> was especially affected,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RedCross_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-RedCross-100">[100]</a></sup> with local schools shut down as a result.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kscafthit_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kscafthit-101">[101]</a></sup> The crisis was reportedly over by early 2012 because of coordinated relief efforts. Aid agencies subsequently shifted their emphasis to recovery initiatives, including digging irrigation canals and distributing plant seeds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Unsfisiobrr_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Unsfisiobrr-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>In August 2010, Kenyans held a <a href="/wiki/2010_Kenyan_constitutional_referendum" title="2010 Kenyan constitutional referendum">referendum</a> and passed <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Kenya" title="Constitution of Kenya">a new constitution</a>, which limited presidential powers and devolved the central government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCKenyaHist_92-2"><a href="#cite_note-BBCKenyaHist-92">[92]</a></sup> Following the passage of the new constitution, Kenya became a <a href="/wiki/Presidential_system" title="Presidential system">presidential</a> <a href="/wiki/Representative_democracy" title="Representative democracy">representative democratic</a> republic, whereby the <a href="/wiki/President_of_Kenya" title="President of Kenya">President of Kenya</a> is both <a href="/wiki/Head_of_state" title="Head of state">head of state</a> and <a href="/wiki/Head_of_government" title="Head of government">head of government</a>, and of a <a href="/wiki/Multi-party_system" title="Multi-party system">multi-party system</a>. The new constitution also states that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Executive_power" title="Executive power">executive powers</a> are exercised by the executive branch of government, headed by the president, who chairs a cabinet composed of people chosen from outside parliament. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Legislative_power" title="Legislative power">Legislative power</a> is vested exclusively in <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Kenya" title="Parliament of Kenya">Parliament</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Judiciary" title="Judiciary">judiciary</a> is independent of the executive and the legislature.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kenyatta_presidency">Kenyatta presidency</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Uhuru_Kenyatta" title="Uhuru Kenyatta">Uhuru Kenyatta</a> and <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Uhuru_Kenyatta" title="Presidency of Uhuru Kenyatta">Presidency of Uhuru Kenyatta</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Uhuru_Kenyatta.png"><img alt="Uhuru Kenyatta in 2014" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="574" data-file-width="514" decoding="async" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Uhuru_Kenyatta.png/220px-Uhuru_Kenyatta.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Uhuru_Kenyatta.png/330px-Uhuru_Kenyatta.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Uhuru_Kenyatta.png/440px-Uhuru_Kenyatta.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Uhuru_Kenyatta.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Uhuru_Kenyatta" title="Uhuru Kenyatta">Uhuru Kenyatta</a> in 2014.</div></div></div><p>After Kibaki's tenure ended in 2013, Kenya held its <a href="/wiki/2013_Kenyan_general_election" title="2013 Kenyan general election">first general elections</a> after the 2010 constitution had been passed. Uhuru Kenyatta won in a disputed election result, leading to a petition by the opposition leader, Raila Odinga. The supreme court upheld the election results and Kenyatta began his term with <a href="/wiki/William_Ruto" title="William Ruto">William Ruto</a> as deputy president. Despite this ruling, the Supreme Court and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_Judge" title="Supreme Court Judge">head of the Supreme Court</a> were seen as powerful institutions that could check the powers of the president.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2017, Kenyatta won a second term in office in another <a href="/wiki/2017_Kenyan_general_election" title="2017 Kenyan general election">disputed election</a>. Odinga again petitioned the results in the Supreme Court, accusing the <a href="/wiki/Independent_Electoral_and_Boundaries_Commission" title="Independent Electoral and Boundaries Commission">Independent Electoral and Boundaries Commission</a> of mismanagement of the elections and Kenyatta and his party of rigging. The Supreme Court overturned the election results in what became a landmark ruling in Africa and one of the very few in the world in which the results of a presidential elections were annulled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> This ruling solidified the position of the Supreme Court as an independent body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Consequently, Kenya had a second round of elections for the presidential position, in which Kenyatta emerged the winner after Odinga refused to participate, citing irregularities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="BBI">BBI</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Kenya#BBI" title="Constitution of Kenya">Constitution of Kenya § BBI</a></div>
<p>In March 2018, a historic <a href="/wiki/2018_Kenya_handshake" title="2018 Kenya handshake">handshake</a> between Kenyatta and his longtime opponent Odinga signaled a period of reconciliation followed by economic growth and increased stability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>Between 2019 and 2021, Kenyatta and Odinga combined efforts to promote major changes to the Kenyan constitution, labelled the "Building Bridges Initiative" (BBI), saying that their efforts were to improve inclusion and overcome the country's winner-take-all election system that often resulted in post-election violence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbi_blocked_2021_05_14_bbc_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbi_blocked_2021_05_14_bbc-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kenyan_court_2021_05_13_reuters_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-kenyan_court_2021_05_13_reuters-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>The BBI proposal called for broad expansion of the legislative and executive branches, including the creation of a <a href="/wiki/Prime_minister" title="Prime minister">prime minister</a> with two deputies and an official leader of the opposition, reverting to selecting cabinet ministers from among the elected Members of Parliament, establishment of up to 70 new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constituencies" title="Constituencies">constituencies</a>, and addition of up to 300 unelected members of Parliament (under an "<a href="/wiki/Affirmative_action" title="Affirmative action">affirmative action</a>" plan).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbi_blocked_2021_05_14_bbc_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-bbi_blocked_2021_05_14_bbc-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kenyan_court_2021_05_13_reuters_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-kenyan_court_2021_05_13_reuters-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>Critics saw this as an unnecessary attempt to reward political dynasties and blunt the efforts of Deputy President <a href="/wiki/William_Ruto" title="William Ruto">Willian Ruto</a> (Odinga's rival for the next presidency) and bloat the government at an exceptional cost to the debt-laded country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbi_blocked_2021_05_14_bbc_110-2"><a href="#cite_note-bbi_blocked_2021_05_14_bbc-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kenyan_court_2021_05_13_reuters_111-2"><a href="#cite_note-kenyan_court_2021_05_13_reuters-111">[111]</a></sup> Ultimately, in May 2021, the Kenyan High Court ruled that the BBI constitutional reform effort was unconstitutional, because it was not truly a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Popular_initiative" title="Popular initiative">popular initiative</a>, but rather an effort of the government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbi_blocked_2021_05_14_bbc_110-3"><a href="#cite_note-bbi_blocked_2021_05_14_bbc-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kenyan_court_2021_05_13_reuters_111-3"><a href="#cite_note-kenyan_court_2021_05_13_reuters-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>The court sharply criticized Kenyatta for the attempt, laying out out grounds for his being sued, personally, or even <a href="/wiki/Impeachment" title="Impeachment">impeached</a> (though the Parliament, which had passed the BBI, was unlikely to do that). The ruling was seen as a major defeat for both Kenyatta (soon to leave office), and Odinga (expected to seek the presidency), but a boon to Odinga's future presidential-election rival, Ruto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbi_blocked_2021_05_14_bbc_110-4"><a href="#cite_note-bbi_blocked_2021_05_14_bbc-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kenyan_court_2021_05_13_reuters_111-4"><a href="#cite_note-kenyan_court_2021_05_13_reuters-111">[111]</a></sup> On 20 August 2021, Kenya's Court of Appeal again upheld the High Court Judgment of May 2021, which was appealed by the BBI Secretariat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ruto_presidency">Ruto presidency</span></h3>
<p>In August 2022, Deputy President William Ruto narrowly won the <a href="/wiki/2022_Kenyan_general_election" title="2022 Kenyan general election">presidential election.</a> He took  50.5% of the vote. His main rival, Raila Odinga, got 48.8% of the vote.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> On 13 September 2022,  William Ruto was  sworn in as Kenya's fifth president.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Kenya" title="Geography of Kenya">Geography of Kenya</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Un-kenya.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3092" data-file-width="2367" decoding="async" height="287" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Un-kenya.png/220px-Un-kenya.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Un-kenya.png/330px-Un-kenya.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Un-kenya.png/440px-Un-kenya.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Un-kenya.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A map of Kenya.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Koppen-Geiger_Map_KEN_present.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="850" data-file-width="1290" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Koppen-Geiger_Map_KEN_present.svg/220px-Koppen-Geiger_Map_KEN_present.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Koppen-Geiger_Map_KEN_present.svg/330px-Koppen-Geiger_Map_KEN_present.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Koppen-Geiger_Map_KEN_present.svg/440px-Koppen-Geiger_Map_KEN_present.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Koppen-Geiger_Map_KEN_present.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="Köppen climate classification">Köppen climate classification</a> map of Kenya.</div></div></div>
<p>At 580,367 km<sup>2</sup> (224,081 sq mi),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cia_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-cia-11">[11]</a></sup> Kenya is the world's 47th-largest country (after <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a>). It lies between latitudes <a href="/wiki/5th_parallel_north" title="5th parallel north">5°N</a> and <a href="/wiki/5th_parallel_south" title="5th parallel south">5°S</a>, and longitudes <a href="/wiki/34th_meridian_east" title="34th meridian east">34°</a> and <a href="/wiki/42nd_meridian_east" title="42nd meridian east">42°E</a>. From the coast on the Indian Ocean, the low plains rise to central highlands. The highlands are bisected by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kenyan_Rift_Valley" title="Kenyan Rift Valley">Great Rift Valley</a>, with a fertile plateau to the east.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The Kenyan Highlands are one of the most successful agricultural production regions in Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-115">[115]</a></sup> The highlands are the site of the highest point in Kenya and the second highest peak on the continent: <a href="/wiki/Mount_Kenya" title="Mount Kenya">Mount Kenya</a>, which reaches a height of 5,199 m (17,057 ft) and is the site of glaciers. <a href="/wiki/Mount_Kilimanjaro" title="Mount Kilimanjaro">Mount Kilimanjaro</a> (5,895 m or 19,341 ft) can be seen from Kenya to the south of the Tanzanian border.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span></h3>
<p>Kenya's climate varies from tropical along the coast to temperate inland to <a href="/wiki/Arid" title="Arid">arid</a> in the north and northeast parts of the country. The area receives a great deal of sunshine every month. It is usually cool at night and early in the morning inland at higher elevations.
</p><p>The "long rains" season occurs from March/April to May/June. The "short rains" season occurs from October to November/December. The rainfall is sometimes heavy and often falls in the afternoons and evenings.
